<p><i><b>The Marshall Mathers LP</b></i> is the third <a href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album" class="mw-redirect">studio album</a> by American rapper <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a>. It was released on May 23, 2000, by <a href="/wiki/Interscope_Records" title="Interscope Records">Interscope Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_Entertainment" title="Aftermath Entertainment">Aftermath Entertainment</a> and his newly founded label, <a href="/wiki/Shady_Records" title="Shady Records">Shady Records</a> in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> and on September 11, 2000, by <a href="/wiki/Polydor_Records" title="Polydor Records">Polydor Records</a> in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Tower2_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tower2-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The album was produced mostly by <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a> and Eminem, along with <a href="/wiki/DJ_Mark_the_45_King" title="DJ Mark the 45 King" class="mw-redirect">The 45 King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_Brothers" title="Bass Brothers">Bass Brothers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mel-Man" title="Mel-Man">Mel-Man</a>. Released a year after Eminem's breakout album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Slim_Shady_LP" title="The Slim Shady LP">The Slim Shady LP</a></i>, the record features more introspective lyricism including the rapper's response to his sudden rise to fame and controversy surrounding his lyrics.</p>
<p>The album sold more than 1.76 million copies in the US in the first week alone, becoming the fastest-selling studio album by any solo artist in American music history.<sup id="cite_ref-Sales_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sales-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2001, the album won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Grammy Award for Best Rap Album</a> and was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Album_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Album of the Year">Album of the Year</a>. The album was certified <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">diamond</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> in 2011 for shipping 10 million copies in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-RIAA_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RIAA-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ifpi_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ifpi-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Since its release in 2000, the album sold 10,766,000 copies in the United States and more than 21 million copies worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-WW_Sales_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WW_Sales-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> has been ranked as one of the greatest hip hop albums of all time by <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-RollingStone_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RollingStone-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-TIME_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIME-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/XXL_(magazine)" title="XXL (magazine)">XXL</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-XXL_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XXL-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GLAAD_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> placed the album at number 7 on its list of the best albums of the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-rollingstone.com_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rollingstone.com-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> The album was ranked number 244 by <i>Rolling Stone</i> on their list of <a href="/wiki/The_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time" class="mw-redirect">The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Rhapsody_(online_music_service)" title="Rhapsody (online music service)">Rhapsody</a> ranked it number 1 on their "The 10 Best Albums by White Rappers" list.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Sequel" title="Sequel">sequel</a> to the album titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marshall_Mathers_LP_2" title="The Marshall Mathers LP 2">The Marshall Mathers LP 2</a></i> was announced during the <a href="/wiki/2013_MTV_Video_Music_Awards" title="2013 MTV Video Music Awards">2013 MTV Video Music Awards</a> and was released on November 5, 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Inspired by the disappointment of his debut studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Infinite_(Eminem_album)" title="Infinite (Eminem album)">Infinite</a></i> (1996), <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Alter_ego" title="Alter ego">alter ego</a> Slim Shady, whom he introduced on <i><a href="/wiki/Slim_Shady_EP" title="Slim Shady EP">Slim Shady EP</a></i> (1997).<sup id="cite_ref-BlowsUp_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlowsUp-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> After placing second in the annual Rap Olympics, Eminem was noticed by the staff at Interscope Records and eventually CEO <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Iovine" title="Jimmy Iovine">Jimmy Iovine</a>, who played <i>Slim Shady EP</i> for rapper <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BlowsUp_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlowsUp-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem and Dr. Dre then recorded <i><a href="/wiki/The_Slim_Shady_LP" title="The Slim Shady LP">The Slim Shady LP</a></i> (1999), which was noted for its over-the-top lyrical depictions of drugs and violence.<sup id="cite_ref-BlowsUp_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlowsUp-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Slim Shady LP</i> became a commercial and critical success, debuting at number two on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> chart and selling 283,000 copies in its first week.<sup id="cite_ref-Charts.3F_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charts.3F-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/41st_Grammy_Awards" title="41st Grammy Awards" class="mw-redirect">41st Grammy Awards</a> in 1999, the record won <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a>, while the album's lead single "<a href="/wiki/My_Name_Is" title="My Name Is">My Name Is</a>" won <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Solo_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Solo Performance">Best Rap Solo Performance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RSbio_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSbio-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Slim Shady LP</i> turned Eminem from an unknown rapper to a high-profile celebrity. The rapper, who had previously struggled to provide for his daughter Hailie, noted a drastic change in his lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_123_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_123-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 1999, he married his girlfriend Kimberly Ann "Kim" Scott, the mother of Hailie, despite the fact that the song "'97 Bonnie &amp; Clyde" from <i>The Slim Shady LP</i> contains references to killing her.<sup id="cite_ref-finalsplit_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finalsplit-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> The rapper became uncomfortable with the level of fame he had achieved, and reflected, "I don't trust nobody now because everybody I meet is meeting me as Eminem...I don't know if they are hanging with me 'cause they like me or because I'm a celebrity or because they think they can get something from me. "<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_123_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_123-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem also became a highly controversial figure due to his lyrical content. He was labeled as "misogynist, a nihilist and an advocate of domestic violence", and in an editorial, <i>Billboard</i> editor in chief <a href="/wiki/Timothy_White_(editor)" title="Timothy White (editor)">Timothy White</a> accused Eminem of "making money by exploiting the world's misery".<sup id="cite_ref-BlowsUp_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlowsUp-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recording">Recording</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Recording">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg/192px-Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg" width="192" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg/288px-Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg/384px-Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="520" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eminem_at_DJ_hero_party_with_d12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.24wmf6/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Eminem (pictured in 2009) wrote the majority of <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> while in the studio.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> was recorded in a two-month-long "creative binge", which often involved 20-hour-long studio sessions.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_153_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_153-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem hoped to keep publicity down during the recording in order to stay focused on working and figuring out how to "map out" each song.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_153_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_153-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> He described himself as a "studio rat" who benefited creatively from the isolated environment of the studio.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_156_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_156-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Much of the album was written spontaneously in the studio; Dr. Dre noted, "We don't wake up at two in the morning, call each other, and say, 'I have an idea. We gotta get to the studio. ' We just wait and see what happens when we get there. "<sup id="cite_ref-Drdrevh1_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drdrevh1-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem observed that much of his favorite material on the album evolved from "fucking around" in the studio; "Marshall Mathers" developed from the rapper watching Jeff Bass casually strumming a guitar, while "Criminal" was based on a piano riff Eminem overheard Bass playing in studio next door.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_156_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_156-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> "Kill You" was written when Eminem heard the track playing in the background while talking to Dr. Dre on the phone and developed an interest in using it for a song. He then wrote the lyrics at home and met up with Dr. Dre and the two recorded the song together.<sup id="cite_ref-Drdrevh1_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drdrevh1-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"Kim" was the first song the rapper recorded for the album, shortly after finishing work on <i>The Slim Shady LP</i> in late 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-hasted120_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hasted120-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem wrote "Kim" at a time in which he and his wife were separated, and he had just watched a romantic movie alone at a theater.<sup id="cite_ref-hasted120_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hasted120-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Originally intending to write a love song for her while using <a href="/wiki/Ecstasy_(drug)" title="Ecstasy (drug)" class="mw-redirect">ecstasy</a>, the rapper hoped to avoid overt sentimentality and thus began writing a song of hate.<sup id="cite_ref-Vanhoozer84_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhoozer84-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> With the track, the rapper aimed to create a short horror story in the form of a song. Once the couple reconciled, Eminem recalls, "I asked her to tell me what she thought of it. I remember my dumb ass saying, 'I know this is a fucked-up song, but it shows how much I care about you. To even think about you this much. To even put you on a song like this'. "<sup id="cite_ref-hasted121_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hasted121-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> The song "<a href="/wiki/Stan_(song)" title="Stan (song)">Stan</a>" was produced by <a href="/wiki/DJ_Mark_the_45_King" title="DJ Mark the 45 King" class="mw-redirect">The 45 King</a>. Eminem's manager, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Rosenberg_(music_manager)" title="Paul Rosenberg (music manager)">Paul Rosenberg</a>, sent Eminem a tape of the producer's beats, and the second track featured a sample of English singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Dido_(singer)" title="Dido (singer)">Dido</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Thank_You_(Dido_song)" title="Thank You (Dido song)">Thank You</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon hearing the song's lyrics, Eminem felt they described an obsessed fan, which became the inspiration for the song. The writing process for "Stan" differed greatly from Eminem's usual strategy, in which song concepts form during the writing: "'Stan' was one of the few songs that I actually sat down and had everything mapped out for. I knew what it was going to be about. "<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Dido later heard "Stan" and enjoyed it, and observed, "I got this letter out of the blue one day. It said, 'We like your album, we've used this track. Hope you don't mind, and hope you like it. ' When they sent ['Stan'] to me and I played it in my hotel room, I was like, 'Wow! This track's amazing. '"<sup id="cite_ref-Dido_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dido-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The record label speculated that Eminem would be the first artist to sell one million copies in an album's first week of release. These expectations placed a large burden on Eminem, who recalled, "I was scared to death. I wanted to be successful, but before anything, I want respect. "<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> After the album was finished, the record label felt that there were no songs that had potential to be a lead single.<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Feeling pressured, Eminem returned to the studio and wrote "The Way I Am" as his way of saying, "Look, this is the best I can do. I can't give you another 'My Name Is. ' I can't just sit in there and make that magic happen. "<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> However, after the song was added to the album, Eminem felt the urge to write another song, and gave a hook to Dr. Dre for him to create a beat, and went home to write new lyrics; the song eventually became "The Real Slim Shady".<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The song also discusses Eminem killing Dr. Dre. The producer reflected, "It was funny to me. As long as it's hot, let's roll with it ... in my opinion, the crazier it is the better. Let's have fun with it and excite people. "<sup id="cite_ref-Drdrevh1_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drdrevh1-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_lyrics">Music and lyrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Music and lyrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Much of the album's first half was produced by Dr. Dre and <a href="/wiki/Mel-Man" title="Mel-Man">Mel-Man</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-produce_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-produce-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> who employed their typical sparse, stripped-down beats, to put more focus on Eminem's vocals. The background music on the record employs "liquid basslines, stuttering rhythms, slight sound effects, and spacious soundscapes".<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_review_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_review-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bass_Brothers" title="Bass Brothers">Bass Brothers</a> and Eminem produced most of the second half,<sup id="cite_ref-produce_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-produce-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> which ranges from the laid-back guitars of "Marshall Mathers" to the atmosphere of "Amityville". The only outside producer on the album was The 45 King, who sampled a verse from Dido's song "Thank You" for "Stan", while adding a slow bass line.<sup id="cite_ref-produce_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-produce-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> contains more autobiographical themes in comparison to <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilburn_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilburn-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Much of the album is spent addressing his rise to fame and attacking those who criticized his previous album. Other themes include his relationship with his family, most notably his mother and Kim Mathers, his former wife.<sup id="cite_ref-divorce_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-divorce-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Unlike Eminem's debut, <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> is more introspective in its lyrics and uses less of the Slim Shady persona, with <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Thomas_Erlewine" title="Stephen Thomas Erlewine">Stephen Thomas Erlewine</a> writing that the album's lyrics "[blur] the distinction between reality and fiction, humor and horror, satire and documentary".<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_review_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_review-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The record showcases a variety of moods, ranging from irreverent and humorous to "dark and unsettling enough to make you want to enlarge the parental warning stickers on the album. "<sup id="cite_ref-Hilburn_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilburn-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Neil_Strauss" title="Neil Strauss">Neil Strauss</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, "Eminem never makes it clear which character—Slim Shady or Marshall Mathers—is the mask and which is the real person, because there is no clear-cut answer, except that there's a little bit of each character in all of us."<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most songs cover Eminem's childhood struggles and family issues, involving his mother ("Kill You"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> the relationship struggles with his wife ("<a href="/wiki/Kim_(song)" title="Kim (song)">Kim</a>"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> his struggles with his superstardom and expectations ("Stan", "I'm Back", &amp; "Marshall Mathers"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> his return and effect on the music industry ("Remember Me?", "Bitch Please II"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> his drug use ("Drug Ballad"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> his effect on the American youth and society ("<a href="/wiki/The_Way_I_Am_(Eminem_song)" title="The Way I Am (Eminem song)">The Way I Am</a>", "Who Knew"),<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> and reactionary barbs to critical response of his vulgarity and dark themes ("Criminal").<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the large amount of controversy regarding the lyrics, the lyrics on the album were overwhelmingly well received among critics and the <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a> community, many praising Eminem's verbal energy and dense rhyme patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-RS_review_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS_review-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-censor_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censor-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The record also contains lyrics that have been considered to be <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">homophobic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-badrap_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badrap-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The song "Criminal" features the line "My words are like a dagger with a jagged edge/That'll stab you in the head whether you're a fag or les...Hate fags?/The answer's yes. "<sup id="cite_ref-badrap_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badrap-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gay_%26_Lesbian_Alliance_Against_Defamation" title="Gay &amp; Lesbian Alliance Against Defamation" class="mw-redirect">Gay &amp; Lesbian Alliance Against Defamation</a> (GLAAD) condemned his lyrics and criticized the album for "encourag[ing] violence against gay men and lesbians".<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted162_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted162-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> However, writing for the <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> interest magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_Advocate" title="The Advocate">The Advocate</a></i>, editor Dave White writes, "If he has gay-bashed you or me, then it logically follows that he has also raped his own mother, killed his wife, and murdered his producer, Dr. Dre. If he's to be taken literally, then so is <a href="/wiki/Britney_Spears" title="Britney Spears">Britney Spears</a>' invitation to '<a href="/wiki/...Baby_One_More_Time" title="...Baby One More Time">hit me baby, one more time</a>'. "<sup id="cite_ref-badrap_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badrap-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem noted that he began using the word "faggot" more frequently when "people got all up in arms about it...to piss them off worse" but added that "I think its hard for some people to understand that for me the word 'faggot' has nothing to do with sexual preference. I meant something more like assholes or dickheads. "<sup id="cite_ref-Eminem54_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminem54-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Songs">Songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa; background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em;">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:2px 0;" class="fn"><a href="/wiki/File:Eminem_-_Stan_(Album_Version).ogg" title="File:Eminem - Stan (Album Version).ogg">"Stan"</a></div>
<div style="padding-right:4px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;height:23px" poster="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.24wmf6/skins/common/images/icons/fileicon-ogg.png" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="30.946394557823" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Eminem_-_Stan_(Album_Version).ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4f/Eminem_-_Stan_%28Album_Version%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (69 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="69291"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />
You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4f/Eminem_-_Stan_%28Album_Version%29.ogg">download the clip</a> or <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">download a player</a> to play the clip in your browser.</audio></div>
</div>
<div style="padding:2px 0 0 0;" class="description">The album's third single has been referred to as the album's centerpiece and features a sample of <a href="/wiki/Dido_(singer)" title="Dido (singer)">Dido</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Thank_You_(Dido_song)" title="Thank You (Dido song)">Thank You</a>". During the verses, Eminem portrays both himself and an obsessive fan, with pen-scratching sounds in the background to indicate communication via letters.</div>
</div>
<hr /></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em;"><span style="font-size:smaller;"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The first track, "Kill You", discusses the controversy that surrounded the rapper's first album, nightmares of "ladies' screams", and being raised by a single mother.<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In the song, Eminem also talks of raping his mother, and "notes the irony of magazines trumpeting his mother-raping self on their covers'. "<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The six-and-a-half minute long "Stan" samples Dido's "Thank You" and tells the story of an exchange between the rapper and an obsessive fan, where the titular character berates Eminem for not responding to his letters.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasted_68_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasted_68-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> "The Way I Am" is a meditation on the pressure to maintain his fame, and his fear of being "pigeon-holed into some poppy sensation/to cop me rotation at rock 'n' roll stations".<sup id="cite_ref-Vanhoozer8384_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhoozer8384-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> He also laments the negative media attention received by controversial public figures such as himself and <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Manson" title="Marilyn Manson">Marilyn Manson</a> in the wake of disasters such as the <a href="/wiki/Columbine_High_School_massacre" title="Columbine High School massacre">Columbine High School massacre</a>. The rapper criticizes the media for focusing on tragedies such as school shootings while ignoring inner-city violence that occurs on a daily basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Vanhoozer8384_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhoozer8384-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> "The Real Slim Shady" pokes fun at pop culture icons such as Spears, <a href="/wiki/Christina_Aguilera" title="Christina Aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Will_Smith" title="Will Smith">Will Smith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis256_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis256-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> "Remember Me?" follows and features rappers <a href="/wiki/RBX" title="RBX">RBX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sticky_Fingaz" title="Sticky Fingaz">Sticky Fingaz</a>, who "kick seriously <a href="/wiki/Stygia_(Conan)" title="Stygia (Conan)" class="mw-redirect">Stygian</a> darkness on the ominous track".<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> "I'm Back" features Eminem's observations regarding his rise to fame, explaining that he "became a commodity/'Cause I'm W-H-I-T-E".<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The next song, "Marshall Mathers" mocks the chorus of <a href="/wiki/LFO_(American_band)" title="LFO (American band)">LFO</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Summer_Girls" title="Summer Girls">Summer Girls</a>", while criticizing the lack of artistic merit of pop stars such as Britney Spears and <a href="/wiki/%27N_Sync" title="'N Sync">'N Sync</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis256_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis256-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ghero_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ghero-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"Drug Ballad" describes the rapper's favorite drugs, including ecstasy which makes him "sentimental as fuck, spilling guts to you/we just met, but I think I'm in love with you".<sup id="cite_ref-xfactor_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xfactor-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> "Amityville" is a bass-heavy ode to living in Detroit, where the rapper discusses the city's crowning as murder capital of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-RS_review_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS_review-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> "Bitch Please II" features Dr. Dre, <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nate_Dogg" title="Nate Dogg">Nate Dogg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xzibit" title="Xzibit">Xzibit</a>, and contains elements of <a href="/wiki/G-funk" title="G-funk">g-funk</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/R%26B" title="R&amp;B" class="mw-redirect">R&amp;B</a> crooning from Nate Dogg on the chorus.<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> "Kim", the prequel to "'97 Bonnie and Clyde" from <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>, features Eminem "screaming at his ex in an insane stream-of-consciousness hate spew".<sup id="cite_ref-RS_review_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS_review-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The song begins with Eminem talking softly to his daughter, but as the beat starts, the rapper takes on portraying two characters, utilizing his own enraged, threatening voice, and the terrified shrieks of his wife Kim.<sup id="cite_ref-Vanhoozer85_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhoozer85-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> As the song ends, Eminem kills her while taunting, "Bleed, bitch, bleed!"<sup id="cite_ref-Vanhoozer85_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhoozer85-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> "Kim" is followed by "Under the Influence", which sees Eminem speaking in gibberish for the chorus, and later rap group <a href="/wiki/D12" title="D12">D12</a> "runs rampant" on the track.<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> "Criminal" features production from Dr. Dre, which consists of "piano licks, swerving synth, and a deceptively simplistic bass rumble over which Em snakes and snarls and warns that 'you can't stop me from topping these charts...'".<sup id="cite_ref-ign_34-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> He pokes fun at critics who take his lyrical content seriously, explaining that "half the shit I say, I just make it up to make you mad".<sup id="cite_ref-sinsandsorrows_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinsandsorrows-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Censored_version">Censored version</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Censored version">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In his book <i>Edited Clean Version: Technology and the Culture of Control</i>, author Raiford Guins writes that the clean version of <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> "resembles a cross between a cell phone chat with terrible reception...and a noted hip-hop lyricist suffering from an incurable case of hiccups. "<sup id="cite_ref-Guins12_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guins12-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> This version of the album often either omits words completely or obscures them with added sound effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Guins12_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guins12-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The clean version of the album did not censor all profanity. Words like "ass," "bitch," "goddamn, "shit" and "<a href="/wiki/Nigga" title="Nigga">nigga</a>" were uncensored. However, on the track "The Real Slim Shady", the words "bitch" and "shit" were censored out, as they used the clean version released for radio. References to violence and weapons were also significantly altered, and the song "Kill You" and "Drug Ballad" are written as "**** You" and "Ballad" on the back cover of the album.<sup id="cite_ref-Guins12_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guins12-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The song "Kim" is removed completely and replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i>-themed "The Kids".<sup id="cite_ref-Bozza83_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bozza83-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> "The Kids" was also featured on limited edition copies of the uncensored album. However, it was only sold in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-censor_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censor-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Significant edits were made to aggressive and violent lyrics that were aimed at police, prostitutes, women, homosexuals, and schools. In response to <a href="/wiki/Columbine_High_School_massacre" title="Columbine High School massacre">the attack</a> that had occurred at <a href="/wiki/Columbine_High_School" title="Columbine High School">Columbine High School</a> in April 1999, names of guns and sounds of them firing were censored. Interscope Records insisted on censoring the words "kids" and "Columbine" from the line "I take seven [kids] from [Columbine], stand them all in line" from "I'm Back", even on the explicit version of the album.<sup id="cite_ref-sinsandsorrows_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinsandsorrows-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Mike Rubin of <i>Spin</i> called the censorship a "curious decision, given that lyrics like 'Take drugs / Rape sluts' are apparently permissible".<sup id="cite_ref-sinsandsorrows_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinsandsorrows-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem commented on his lyrics regarding the shooting, "That Columbine shit is so fucking touchy. As much sympathy as we give the Columbine shootings, nobody ever looked at it from the fuckin' point of view of the kids who were bullied-I mean, they took their own fucking life! And it was because they were pushed so far to the fucking edge that they were fucking so mad. I've <i>been</i> that mad. "<sup id="cite_ref-sinsandsorrows_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinsandsorrows-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The full line appears uncensored in Eminem's song "<a href="/wiki/Rap_God" title="Rap God">Rap God</a>" from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marshall_Mathers_LP_2" title="The Marshall Mathers LP 2">The Marshall Mathers LP 2</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The line "It doesn't matter [your attorney Fred Gibson's a] faggot" was also censored from "Marshall Mathers", which refers to his mother Debbie Nelson's lawyer, who assisted her in filing a lawsuit against the rapper for defamation regarding lyrics from <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-emvsmom_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emvsmom-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The intro track "Public Service Announcement 2000" is replaced with two seconds of silence on the clean version of the album.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_and_artwork">Title and artwork</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Title and artwork">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Eminem considered naming the album <i>Amsterdam</i> after a trip to <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">the city</a> shortly after the release of <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>, in which he and his friends engaged in heavy drug use.<sup id="cite_ref-Eminem66_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminem66-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The "free" use of drugs Eminem observed during his time in Amsterdam greatly influenced his desire to openly discuss drug use in his music and inspired some of the content on the album.<sup id="cite_ref-Eminem66_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminem66-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> was released with two different album covers. The original features Eminem sitting on the porch of the house he lived in during his teenage years.<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> He reflected on the photo shoot by saying, "I had mixed feelings because I had a lot of good and bad memories in that house. But to go back to where I grew up and finally say, 'I've made it', is the greatest feeling in the world to me. "<sup id="cite_ref-Eminemvh1_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eminemvh1-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The other cover features the rapper seated in a fetal position beneath a <a href="/wiki/Loading_dock" title="Loading dock">loading dock</a> with alcohol and prescription pill bottles at his feet.<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Will Hermes of <i>Entertainment Weekly</i> likened Eminem's appearance on the cover to a "dysfunctional <a href="/wiki/Our_Gang" title="Our Gang">Little Rascal</a>", viewing the image as indicative of the rapper's musical evolution: "Easy to read, right? The debut: a violent fantasy, the acting-out of a persona. The follow-up: the vulnerable artist unmasked. "<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_performance">Commercial performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Commercial performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> sold 1.76 million copies in its first week, which made it the fastest-selling rap album in history. It sold twice as much as the previous record holder, <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a>'s 1993 album <i><a href="/wiki/Doggystyle" title="Doggystyle">Doggystyle</a></i>, and also topped <a href="/wiki/Britney_Spears" title="Britney Spears">Britney Spears</a>' record for highest one-week sales by any solo artist.<sup id="cite_ref-Sales_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sales-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> The album sold 800,000 in its second week, 598,000 in its third week, and 519,000 copies in its fourth week for a four week total of 3.65 million. It also became one of the few albums to sell over half a million copies for four consecutive weeks. By the end of 2000, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> had become the second highest-selling album of the year with over 7.9 million sold.<sup id="cite_ref-Soundscan_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soundscan-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's first single, "<a href="/wiki/The_Real_Slim_Shady" title="The Real Slim Shady">The Real Slim Shady</a>", became Eminem's biggest hit up to that point and peaked at number four on the <i>Billboard</i> <a href="/wiki/Hot_100" title="Hot 100" class="mw-redirect">Hot 100</a> and topping the UK Singles Charts.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The Way I Am which was released as albums second single peaked at number 8 on the UK Singles Chart<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> and number 58 on the Billboard Hot 100. "Stan", the third single released from the album, became a number-one hit in both the United Kingdom<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> "I'm Back" was released as a single on April 3, 2001, in France,<sup id="cite_ref-ImBackFrance_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImBackFrance-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> where it charted at number 49.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, the Nielsen Company reported that up until November 2009, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> had sold 10,216,000 copies in the US, making it the fourth-best selling album of the decade.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> By February 2014, the album has sold 10,818,000 copies in the United States, being Eminem's best selling album in his native country.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable infobox" style="float: right; width: 24.2em; font-size: 80%; text-align: center; margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em; padding:0;" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">
<tr>
<th scope="col" colspan="2" style="font-size:120%;">Professional ratings</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col" colspan="2" style="background:#d1dbdf; font-size:120%;">Review scores</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Source</th>
<th scope="col">Rating</th>

<td scope="row"><i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stone_Album_Guide" title="The Rolling Stone Album Guide">The Rolling Stone Album Guide</a></i></td>
<td style="vertical-align:middle"><span style="white-space:nowrap" title="5/5 stars"><img alt="5/5 stars" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" data-file-width="108" data-file-height="110" /><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="108" data-file-height="110" /><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="108" data-file-height="110" /><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="108" data-file-height="110" /></span><sup id="cite_ref-RSguide_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSguide-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td scope="row"><i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i></td>
<td style="vertical-align:middle">6/10<sup id="cite_ref-Spin_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spin-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> received generally positive reviews from <a href="/wiki/Music_journalism" title="Music journalism">music critics</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which assigns a <a href="/wiki/Standard_score" title="Standard score">normalized</a> rating out of 100 to reviews from mainstream critics, the album received an <a href="/wiki/Weighted_mean" title="Weighted mean" class="mw-redirect">average</a> score of 78, based on 21 reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-metacritic_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metacritic-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine's <a href="/wiki/Tour%C3%A9" title="Touré">Touré</a> complimented Dr. Dre's production and Eminem's varied lyrical style on what is a "car-crash record: loud, wild, dangerous, out of control, grotesque, unsettling", but ultimately captivating.<sup id="cite_ref-RS_review_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS_review-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i> said that Eminem's startlingly intense vision of "rap's self-consciousness" is truly unique,<sup id="cite_ref-MelodyMaker_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MelodyMaker-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> praised the album as a <a href="/wiki/Misanthropy" title="Misanthropy">misanthropic</a> and "gruelling assault course of lyrical genius" that critiques malevolent aspects of contemporary society.<sup id="cite_ref-NME_review_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NME_review-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Eddy" title="Chuck Eddy">Chuck Eddy</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> said that Eminem is backed by attractive music and displays an emotionally complex and witting quality unlike his previous work.<sup id="cite_ref-Voice_review_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voice_review-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> In his consumer guide for the newspaper, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> called him "exceptionally witty and musical, discernibly thoughtful and good-hearted, indubitably dangerous and full of shit", while declaring the album "a work of art whose immense entertainment value in no way compromises its intimations of a pathology that's both personal and political".<sup id="cite_ref-Christgau_review_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christgau_review-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Will_Hermes" title="Will Hermes">Will Hermes</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> wrote that as the first significant <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> album of the 2000s, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> is "indefensible and critic-proof, hypocritical and heartbreaking, unlistenable and undeniable".<sup id="cite_ref-EW_review_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EW_review-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, music journalist <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kot" title="Greg Kot">Greg Kot</a> said that reaction to the album was "mixed" and reluctantly positive among critics who praised Eminem's "verbal skills and <a href="/wiki/Transgressive_art" title="Transgressive art">transgressive</a> humor", but decried some of the subject matter.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> In his review for the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hilburn" title="Robert Hilburn">Robert Hilburn</a> reserved his praise because of homophobic lyrics on what he felt is an otherwise conceptual and personal work, "docked a half star because of the recurring homophobia—something that may be <a href="/wiki/De_rigueur" title="De rigueur" class="mw-redirect">de rigueur</a> in commercial rap, but which still is unacceptable."<sup id="cite_ref-Hilburn_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilburn-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)" class="mw-redirect">Q</a></i> magazine felt that the subject matter does not make for an enjoyable listen, even though Eminem's disaffected and nihilistic lyrics can be provocative.<sup id="cite_ref-Qmag_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qmag-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slant_Magazine" title="Slant Magazine">Slant Magazine</a>'s Sal Cinquemani was more critical in a one-and-a-half star review and found his raps extremely distasteful: "The only thing worse than Eminem's homophobia is the immaturity with which he displays it".<sup id="cite_ref-Slant_review_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slant_review-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> On the other hand, <i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i> felt that the rapping is excellent, but plagued more so by unremarkable music and lackluster tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-Spin_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spin-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2000, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> won in the Best Album category at the <a href="/wiki/MTV_Europe_Music_Awards" title="MTV Europe Music Awards">MTV Europe Music Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MTV_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MTV-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> It also won in the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a> category at the <a href="/wiki/43rd_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="43rd Annual Grammy Awards">43rd Annual Grammy Awards</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-CBC_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBC-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retrospective_acclaim">Retrospective acclaim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Retrospective acclaim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2003, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> was ranked number 302 on <i>Rolling Stone</i><span style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s list of <a href="/wiki/The_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time" class="mw-redirect">the 500 greatest albums of all time</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-RollingStone_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RollingStone-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> it was moved up to number 244 in the magazine's revised 2012 edition of the list.<sup id="cite_ref-RollingStone_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RollingStone-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a> named it the twenty-fourth greatest rap album of all time in a 2004 list.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Sputnikmusic" title="Sputnikmusic">Sputnikmusic</a>'s Nick Butler, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> stands as a culturally significant record in American popular music, but also "remains a truly special album, unique in rap's canon, owing its spirit to rock and its heritage to rap, in a way I've rarely heard".<sup id="cite_ref-Sputnikmusic_review_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sputnikmusic_review-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stone_Album_Guide" title="The Rolling Stone Album Guide">The Rolling Stone Album Guide</a></i> (2004), Cheo Hoker said that the album "delved much deeper into personal pain [than <i>The Slim Shady LP</i>], and the result was a minor masterpiece that merged iller-than-<a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ill" class="extiw" title="wikt:ill">ill</a> <a href="/wiki/Flow_(music)" title="Flow (music)" class="mw-redirect">flows</a> with a brilliant sense of the <a href="/wiki/Macabre" title="Macabre">macabre</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-RSguide_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSguide-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> was chosen by <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> as one of the 100 greatest albums of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-TIME_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIME-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> That same year, <i>Q</i> ranked it number 85 on a list of the greatest albums of all time, the highest position held by any rap album on the list.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> was also the highest ranked rap album on the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Recording_Merchandisers" title="National Association of Recording Merchandisers" class="mw-redirect">National Association of Recording Merchandisers</a> &amp; the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a>'s list of the 200 greatest albums of all time, where it was placed at number 28.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been named one of the greatest albums of the 2000s decade by <i>Rolling Stone</i>, who ranked it seventh,<sup id="cite_ref-rollingstone.com_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rollingstone.com-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i>, who ranked it fourth,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_Media" title="Pitchfork Media">Pitchfork Media</a>, who ranked it 119th.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Marshall_Mathers_LP&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Misogyny_in_hip_hop_culture" title="Misogyny in hip hop culture">Misogyny in hip hop culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:175px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrscheney.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Mrscheney.jpeg/173px-Mrscheney.jpeg" width="173" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Mrscheney.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Mrscheney.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="231" data-file-height="294" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrscheney.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.24wmf6/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lynne_Cheney" title="Lynne Cheney">Lynne Cheney</a> called for age-restrictions on music sales after citing lyrics from "Kill You" from <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> hearing, former chairman <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Cheney" title="Lynne Cheney">Lynne Cheney</a> criticized Eminem and sponsor <a href="/wiki/Seagram" title="Seagram">Seagram</a> for "promot[ing] violence of the most degrading kind against women", labeling him as "a rap singer who advocates murder and rape".<sup id="cite_ref-hearing_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hearing-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> In her claim, she specifically cited lyrics from "Kill You", explaining, "He talks about murdering and raping his mother. He talks about choking women slowly so he can hear their screams for a long time. He talks about using O.J.'s machete on women, and this is a man who is honored by the recording industry".<sup id="cite_ref-hearing_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hearing-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Cheney drew a link between the Columbine massacre and violent music, mentioning artists Eminem and Marilyn Manson as musicians that contribute to the United States' culture of violence. Although she stated that she has "long been a vocal supporter of free speech", Cheney called for the music industry to impose age-restrictions on those who can purchase music with violent content.<sup id="cite_ref-hearing_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hearing-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On October 26, 2000, Eminem was to perform at a concert in Toronto's <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Skydome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-B_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Jim_Flaherty" title="Jim Flaherty">Jim Flaherty</a> argued that Canada should stop Eminem at the border. "I personally don't want anyone coming to Canada who will come here and advocate <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a>", he said.<sup id="cite_ref-B_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Flaherty claims to have been "disgusted" when reading transcriptions of Eminem's song "Kill You", which includes lines like "Slut, you think I won't choke no whore/till the vocal cords don't work in her throat no more?"<sup id="cite_ref-B_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem's fans argued that this was a matter of <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech" class="mw-redirect">free speech</a> and that he was unfairly singled out.<sup id="cite_ref-B_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bryant_(politician)" title="Michael Bryant (politician)">Michael Bryant</a> suggested that the government let Eminem perform and then prosecute him for violating Canada's <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">hate crime</a> laws, despite the fact that Canada's hate-crime legislation does not include violence against women.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> In a <i><a href="/wiki/Globe_and_Mail" title="Globe and Mail" class="mw-redirect">Globe and Mail</a></i> editorial, author Robert Everett-Green wrote, "Being offensive is Eminem's job description."<sup id="cite_ref-A_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem was granted entry into Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A 2001 and 2004 study by Edward Armstrong found that of the 14 songs on <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> eleven contain violent and misogynistic lyrics and nine depict killing women through choking, stabbing, drowning, shooting, head and throat splitting. According to the study, Eminem scores 78% for violent misogyny while <a href="/wiki/Gangsta_rap" title="Gangsta rap">gangsta rap</a> music in general reaches 22%.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-armstrong2_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong2-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Armstrong argues that violent misogyny characterizes most of Eminem's music and that the rapper "authenticates his self-presentations by outdoing other gangsta rappers in terms of his violent misogyny. "<sup id="cite_ref-armstrong2_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong2-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Protests against the album's content reached a climax when it was nominated for four <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards" title="Grammy Awards" class="mw-redirect">Grammy Awards</a> in 2001 including <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Album_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Album of the Year">Album of the Year</a>, marking the first time a <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_rap" title="Hardcore rap" class="mw-redirect">hardcore rap</a> album was ever nominated in this category.<sup id="cite_ref-GLAAD_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> At the ceremony, Eminem performed "Stan" in a duet with openly gay artist <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> playing piano and singing the chorus, as a response to claims by GLAAD and others who claimed his lyrics were homophobic. GLAAD did not change its position, however, and spoke out against Elton John's decision.<sup id="cite_ref-EltonJohn_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EltonJohn-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite significant protests and debate, <i>The Marshall Mathers LP</i> went on to win <a href="/wiki/Best_Rap_Album" title="Best Rap Album" class="mw-redirect">Best Rap Album</a>.</p>
<p>Singer <a href="/wiki/Christina_Aguilera" title="Christina Aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a> was upset about the lyric from "Christina Aguilera better switch me chairs so I can sit next to Carson Daly and Fred Durst / and hear 'em argue over who she gave head to first" from "The Real Slim Shady", calling the rapper's claim "disgusting, offensive and, above all, not true".<sup id="cite_ref-happybday_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-happybday-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Eminem included this line after becoming angry with the singer for informing the public during an MTV special without his consent about the rapper's secret marriage to Kim Mathers. In response to the lyric, Aguilera released a song entitled "Will The Real Slim Shady Please Shut Up", in which she called the rapper a "punk kid trying to be hardcore".<sup id="cite_ref-happybday_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-happybday-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the two later settled their differences after hugging backstage at the <a href="/wiki/2002_MTV_Video_Music_Awards" title="2002 MTV Video Music Awards">2002 MTV Video Music Awards</a>, with the singer appearing at the premiere of <i><a href="/wiki/8_Mile_(film)" title="8 Mile (film)">8 Mile</a></i> months later.<sup id="cite_ref-happybday_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-happybday-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> pianist <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Loussier" title="Jacques Loussier">Jacques Loussier</a> filed a $10 million lawsuit against Eminem, claiming the beat for "Kill You" was stolen from his song.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<tr>
<th class="tlheader" scope="col" style="width: 2em; padding-left: 10px; padding-right: 10px; text-align: right; background-color: #eee">No.</th>

<dt>Sample credits</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>"Kill You" contains an interpolation of "Pulsion" performed by Jacques Loussier.</li>
<li>"Stan" contains a sample of the recording "<a href="/wiki/Thank_You_(Dido_song)" title="Thank You (Dido song)">Thank You</a>", as performed by Dido.</li>
</ul>
